<p>Module Competency</p>
<p>This module is designed to enable the learner to diagnose and manage diseases.</p>
<p>Module Description</p>
<p>This is a level one module suitably done after you have done components of Chemistry, Biology and English</p>
<p>Module Units</p>
<ol>
<li>Biomolecules</li>
<li>Metabolic pathways</li>
<li>Hormones</li>
<li>Enzymes</li>
</ol>
<p>LearningOutcomes</p>
<p>By the end of this module, you will have gained sufficient skills to:</p>
<ol>
<li>Describe biomolecules.</li>
<li>Relate metabolic pathways of biomolecules to health disorders.</li>
<li>Describe the structure, classification and function of hormones.</li>
<li>Apply the mechanisms of enzyme action</li>
</ol>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="introduction">Introduction<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#introduction" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="what-are-biomolecules">What Are Biomolecules?<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#what-are-biomolecules" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Biomolecules are the primary building blocks of every living organism. Together, they promote different biological processes, which are necessary for life. They vary in structures and sizes. Biomolecules primarily consist of carbon, hydrogen and oxygen.</p>
<p>The living systems synthesize four primary types of biomolecules within the body. They are:</p>
<ul>
<li>Carbohydrates</li>
<li>Proteins</li>
<li>Fats/Lipids</li>
<li>Nucleic Acids (DNA &amp; RNA)</li>
</ul>
<p>With biomolecules definition, it is also important to know about the major types of biomolecules are basically polymers of simple elements. For example:</p>
<ul>
<li>Amino acids form the basis for proteins.</li>
<li>Phosphate, sugar, and nitrogen for nucleic acids.</li>
<li>Fatty acids and glycerol for lipids /Fats</li>
<li>Simple sugar for carbohydrates.</li>
</ul>
<p>Some biomolecules can also be combined to get specialized molecules. For example:</p>
<ul>
<li>Protein and carbohydrate in glycoproteins</li>
<li>Protein and lipid in lipoproteins</li>
<li>Lipid and carbohydrate in glycolipids</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="biochemistry">BIOCHEMISTRY<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#biochemistry" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p><strong>Definition</strong>: Biochemistry is the study of the structure and function of biological biomolecules such as proteins, nucleic acids, carbohydrates, lipids among others.</p>
<p>Practitioners of biochemistry study the biochemical reactions that occur at the molecular level within living organisms. In medical biochemistry (also known as molecular biology), biochemical techniques are applied to human health and disease.</p>
<p>Downloaded by PATRICK NGOBIRO (<a href="mailto:patrickngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">patrickngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>Application: Much of biochemical study deals with the structures, functions and interactions of biological macromolecules  <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">♠</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\spadesuit</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.8241em; vertical-align: -0.1296em;"></span><span class="mord">♠</span></span></span></span></span>  large molecules (such as proteins) which provide the structure of cells and perform many of the functions associated with life.</p>
<p>Medicine is just one area that has benefited from biochemistry. The discipline has made vital contributions to the fields of cell biology, physiology, pathology, pharmacology, microbiology, immunology, nutrition, forensic medicine, and toxicology, as well as the fields of inflammation, cell injury, and cancer.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="unit-1-biomolecules">Unit 1: Biomolecules<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#unit-1-biomolecules" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="topic-1-amino-acids-protein">Topic 1: Amino acids &amp; protein<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#topic-1-amino-acids-protein" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="b-topic-objectives">b) Topic Objectives<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#b-topic-objectives" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ol>
<li>Demonstrate understanding of the structure and functions of Amino acids</li>
<li>Illustrate the structures of amino acids</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="amino-acids">Amino acids<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#amino-acids" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Amino acids are organic compounds that contain amine  <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><mo>∙</mo><msub><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">N</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">H</mi></mrow><mn>2</mn></msub><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">(\bullet \mathrm{NH}_2)</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 1em; vertical-align: -0.25em;"></span><span class="mopen">(</span><span class="mord">∙</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathrm">NH</span></span><span class="msupsub"><span class="vlist-t vlist-t2"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.3011em;"><span style="top: -2.55em; margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 2.7em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mtight">2</span></span></span></span><span class="vlist-s">​</span></span><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.15em;"><span></span></span></span></span></span></span><span class="mclose">)</span></span></span></span></span>  and carboxyl  <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><mo>∙</mo><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">C</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">O</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">O</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">H</mi></mrow><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">(\bullet \mathrm{COOH})</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 1em; vertical-align: -0.25em;"></span><span class="mopen">(</span><span class="mord">∙</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathrm">COOH</span></span><span class="mclose">)</span></span></span></span></span>  functional groups, along with a side chain (R) specific to each amino acid. (illustration of an amino acid molecule)</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251216-1c7b04b3.jpeg" alt="img-1.jpeg"></p>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id=""><span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"></span></h1></div>
<p>studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by PATRICK NGOBIRO (<a href="mailto:patrickngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">patrickngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>The key elements of an amino acid are carbon, hydrogen, oxygen, and nitrogen, although other elements such as sulphur are found in the side chains of certain amino acids.</p>
<p>There are 20 different amino acids that can occur within a protein; the order in which they occur plays a fundamental role in determining <strong>protein</strong> structure and function.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="functions-of-proteins">Functions of proteins<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#functions-of-proteins" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ol>
<li>Proteins themselves are major structural elements of cells (body building and repair)</li>
<li>They serve as transporters, moving nutrients and other molecules in and out of cells (oncotic pressure)</li>
<li>They serve as <strong>enzymes</strong> and <strong>catalysts</strong> for the vast majority of <strong>chemical reactions</strong> that take place in living organisms.</li>
<li>Proteins also form <strong>antibodies</strong> and <strong>hormones</strong></li>
<li>They influence <strong>gene</strong> activity</li>
</ol>
<p>All of the proteins on the face of the earth are made up of the same 20 amino acids. Linked together in long chains called polypeptides, amino acids are the building blocks for the vast assortment of proteins found in all living cells.</p>
<p>The twenty amino acids (that make up proteins) each have been assigned three-letter code;</p>
<ol>
<li>alanine - ala</li>
<li>arginine - arg</li>
<li>asparagine - asn</li>
<li>aspartic acid - asp</li>
<li>cysteine - cys</li>
<li>glutamine - gln</li>
<li>glutamic acid - glu</li>
<li>glycine - gly</li>
<li>histidine - his</li>
<li>isoleucine - ile</li>
</ol>
<p>Downloaded by PATRICK NGOBIRO (<a href="mailto:patrickngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">patrickngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ol start="11">
<li>leucine - leu</li>
<li>lysine - lys</li>
<li>methionine - met</li>
<li>phenylalanine - phe</li>
<li>proline - pro</li>
<li>serine - ser</li>
<li>threonine - thr</li>
<li>tryptophan - trp</li>
<li>tyrosine - tyr</li>
<li>valine - val</li>
</ol>
<p>All amino acids have the same basic structure. At the center" of each amino acid is a carbon called <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mi>α</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\alpha</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.4306em;"></span><span class="mord mathnormal" style="margin-right: 0.0037em;">α</span></span></span></span></span> carbon. Attached to it are four groups; a hydrogen, <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mi>α</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\alpha</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.4306em;"></span><span class="mord mathnormal" style="margin-right: 0.0037em;">α</span></span></span></span></span>- carboxyl group, an <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mi>α</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\alpha</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.4306em;"></span><span class="mord mathnormal" style="margin-right: 0.0037em;">α</span></span></span></span></span>-amine group, and an R-group, sometimes referred to as a side chain (illustrated).</p>
<p>The <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mi>α</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\alpha</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.4306em;"></span><span class="mord mathnormal" style="margin-right: 0.0037em;">α</span></span></span></span></span> carbon, carboxyl, and amino groups are common to all amino acids, so the R-group is the only unique feature in each amino acid. With the exception of glycine, which has an R-group consisting of a hydrogen atom, all of the amino acids in proteins have four different groups attached to them and consequently can exist in two mirror image forms, L and D. With only very minor exceptions, every amino acid found in cells and in proteins is in the L configuration.</p>
<p>General basic structure of amino acid</p>
<p>This document is available on</p>

<p>studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by PATRICK NGOBIRO (<a href="mailto:patrickngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">patrickngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251216-f5208cae.jpeg" alt="img-2.jpeg"></p>
<p>All amino acids found in proteins have this basic structure, differing only in the structure of the R-group or the side chain.</p>
<p>The simplest, and smallest, amino acid found in proteins is glycine for which the R-group is hydrogen (H).</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="peptides-and-proteins">Peptides and Proteins<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#peptides-and-proteins" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Peptides are chains of amino acids. Two amino acid molecules can be covalently joined through a substituted amide linkage, termed a peptide bond, to yield a dipeptide. Such a linkage is formed by removal of the elements of water (dehydration) from the -carboxyl group of one amino acid and the amino group of another.</p>
<p>Peptide bond formation is an example of a condensation reaction, a common class of reactions in living cells.</p>
<p>Proteins are composed of tens to (usually) hundreds of amino acids, each linked in a chain by a peptide bond. Peptide bonds are formed by enzymes which have that specific job. The making of a peptide bond is a condensation reaction, because a molecule of water is formed in the process. It looks like this:</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251216-5809cc92.jpeg" alt="img-3.jpeg"></p>
<p>Three amino acids can be joined by two peptide bonds to form a tripeptide; similarly, four amino acids can be linked to form tetra peptides, five to form penta peptides, and so forth.</p>
<p>When a few amino acids are joined in this fashion, the structure is called an oligopeptide.</p>
<p>Downloaded by PATRICK NGOBIRO (<a href="mailto:patrickngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">patrickngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>When many amino acids are joined, the product is called a polypeptide.</p>
<p>Proteins may have thousands of amino acid residues. Although the terms ☰ protein ☰ and “polypeptide ☰ are sometimes used interchangeably, molecules referred to as polypeptides generally have molecular weights below 10,000, and those called proteins have higher molecular weights.</p>
<p>Although hydrolysis of a peptide bond is an exergenic reaction, it occurs only slowly because it has high activation energy.</p>
<p>As a result, the peptide bonds in proteins are quite stable, with an average half-life (t₁/₂) of about 7 years under most intracellular conditions.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="levels-of-protein-structure">Levels of Protein Structure<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#levels-of-protein-structure" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Structural features of proteins are usually described at four levels of complexity:</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="primary-structure">Primary structure:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#primary-structure" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Proteins are made up of polypeptide chains, which are amino acids joined together with peptide bonds. The unique sequence of amino acids that make up a protein or polypeptide chain is called the <strong>Primary Structure</strong>.</p>
<p>Peptide bonds are created by <strong>enzyme catalyzed</strong> condensation reactions and broken down by <strong>enzyme catalyzed</strong> hydrolysis reactions. Breaking down proteins is important in many areas of the body, not merely in digestion. For example, in hormone regulation, cells that are targeted by hormones contain enzymes to break down those hormones. This stops their effects from being permanent and allows them to be controlled.</p>
<p>The primary structure is usually shown using abbreviations for the amino acid residues. These abbreviations commonly consist of three letters or one letter.</p>

<p>Downloaded by PATRICK NGOBIRO (<a href="mailto:patrickngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">patrickngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>Downloaded by PATRICK NGOBIRO (<a href="mailto:patrickngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">patrickngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="primary-structure">Primary Structure<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#primary-structure" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251216-5eb7da87.jpeg" alt="img-4.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="secondary-structure">Secondary structure:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#secondary-structure" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>After synthesis, polypeptide chains are folded or pleated into different shapes, called their Secondary Structure. Two common examples of secondary structures are Alpha Helices and Beta Pleated Sheets.</p>
<p>Secondary structure is held together by many hydrogen bonds, overall giving the shape great stability.</p>
<p>Areas of folding or coiling within a protein; examples include alpha helices and β pleated sheets, which are stabilized by hydrogen bonding.</p>
<p>Within the long protein chains there are regions in which the chains are organized into regular structures known as alpha-helices (alpha-helixes) and beta-pleated sheets. These are the secondary structures in proteins.</p>
<p>These secondary structures are held together by hydrogen bonds. These form as shown in the diagram between one of the lone pairs on an oxygen atom and the hydrogen attached to a nitrogen atom:</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251216-dc5d9eb1.jpeg" alt="img-5.jpeg"></p>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<p>8</p>
<p>studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by PATRICK NGOBIRO (<a href="mailto:patrickngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">patrickngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>Although the hydrogen bonds are always between C=O and H-N groups, the exact pattern of them is different in an alpha-helix and a beta-pleated sheet.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="the-alpha-helix">The alpha-helix<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#the-alpha-helix" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>In an alpha-helix, the protein chain is coiled like a loosely-coiled spring. The "alpha" means that if you look down the length of the spring, the coiling is happening in a clockwise direction as it goes away from you.</p>
<p>So, what do you need to notice?</p>
<ol>
<li>Notice that all the "R" groups are sticking out sideways from the main helix.</li>
<li>Notice the regular arrangement of the hydrogen bonds. All the N-H groups are pointing upwards, and all the C=O groups pointing downwards. Each of them is involved in a hydrogen bond.</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="beta-pleated-sheets">Beta-pleated sheets<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#beta-pleated-sheets" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>In a beta-pleated sheet, the chains are folded so that they lie alongside each other. Thus forming what is known as an "anti-parallel" sheet. That is, the next-door chains are heading in opposite directions</p>
<p>The folded chains are again held together by hydrogen bonds involving exactly the same groups as in the alpha-helix.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="tertiary-structure">Tertiary structure:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#tertiary-structure" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The final three - dimensional structure of a protein, which results from a large number of non-covalent interactions between amino acids.</p>
<p>Tertiary structure is held together by four different bonds and interactions:</p>
<ul>
<li>Disulphide Bonds - Where two Cysteine amino acids are found together, a strong double bond (S=S) is formed between the Sulphur atoms within the Cysteine monomers.</li>
<li>Ionic Bonds - If two oppositely charged  <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><msub><mi mathvariant="bold">R</mi><mtext>三</mtext></msub></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\mathbf{R}_{\text{三}}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.8361em; vertical-align: -0.15em;"></span><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathbf">R</span><span class="msupsub"><span class="vlist-t vlist-t2"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.3283em;"><span style="top: -2.55em; margin-left: 0em; margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 2.7em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mtight"><span class="mord text mtight"><span class="mord cjk_fallback mtight">三</span></span></span></span></span></span><span class="vlist-s">​</span></span><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.15em;"><span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span>  groups (+ve and -ve) are found close to each other, and ionic bond forms between them.</li>
<li>Hydrogen Bonds - The typical Hydrogen bonds.</li>
</ul>
<p>Downloaded by PATRICK NGOBIRO (<a href="mailto:patrickngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">patrickngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>Hydrophobic and Hydrophilic Interactions - Some amino acids may be hydrophobic while others are hydrophilic. In a water based environment, a globular protein will orientate itself such that it's hydrophobic parts are towards its centre and its hydrophilic parts are towards its edges</li>
</ul>
<p>Proteins with a 3D structure fall into two main types:</p>
<ul>
<li>Globular - These tend to form ball-like structures where hydrophobic parts are towards the centre and hydrophilic are towards the edges, which makes them water soluble. They usually have metabolic roles, for example: enzymes in all organisms, plasma proteins and antibodies in mammals.</li>
<li>Fibrous - The proteins form long fibres and mostly consist of repeated sequences of amino acids which are insoluble in water. They usually have structural roles, such as: collagen in bone and cartilage, keratin in fingernails and hair.</li>
</ul>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="quaternary-structure">Quaternary structure:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#quaternary-structure" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Non-covalent interactions that bind multiple polypeptides into a single, larger protein.</p>
<p>Hemoglobin has quaternary structure due to association of two alpha globin and two beta globin polyproteins.</p>
<p>Downloaded by PATRICK NGOBIRO (<a href="mailto:patrickngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">patrickngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>Some proteins are made up of multiple polypeptide chains, sometimes with an inorganic component (for example, a haem group in haemoglobin) called a Prosthetic Group. These proteins will only be able to function if all subunits are present.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251216-a4030aa7.jpeg" alt="img-7.jpeg"></p>

<p>Downloaded by PATRICK NGOBIRO (<a href="mailto:patrickngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">patrickngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="functions-of-amino-acids">Functions of amino acids;<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#functions-of-amino-acids" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ol>
<li>Proteins are known to be the building blocks for all living things.</li>
<li>The linear sequence of amino acid residues in a polypeptide chain determines the three-dimensional configuration of a protein, and the structure of a protein determines its function.</li>
<li>Amino acids are imperative for sustaining the health of the human body.</li>
</ol>
<p>They largely promote the:
i) Production of hormones
ii) Structure of muscles
iii) Human nervous system's healthy functioning
iv) The health of vital organs
v) Normal cellular structure</p>
<ol start="4">
<li>The amino acids are used by various tissues to synthesize proteins and to produce nitrogen-containing compounds (e.g., purines, heme, creatine, epinephrine), or they are oxidized to produce energy.</li>
<li>The breakdown of both dietary and tissue proteins yields nitrogen-containing substrates and carbon skeletons.</li>
<li>The nitrogen-containing substrates are used in the biosynthesis of purines, pyrimidines, neurotransmitters, hormones, porphyrins, and nonessential amino acids.</li>
<li>The carbon skeletons are used as a fuel source in the citric acid cycle, used for gluconeogenesis, or used in fatty acid synthesis.</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="properties-of-amino-acids">Properties of amino acids<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#properties-of-amino-acids" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="physical-properties">Physical Properties<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#physical-properties" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ol>
<li>
<p>Amino acids are colourless, crystalline solid.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>All amino acids have a high melting point greater than <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><msup><mn>200</mn><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">∘</mo></msup></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">200^{\circ}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6741em;"></span><span class="mord">20</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord">0</span><span class="msupsub"><span class="vlist-t"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.6741em;"><span style="top: -3.063em; margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 2.7em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mtight"><span class="mord mtight">∘</span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Solubility: They are soluble in water, slightly soluble in alcohol and dissolve with difficulty in methanol, ethanol, and propanol. R-group of amino acids and pH of the solvent play important role in solubility.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>On heating to high temperatures, they decompose.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>All amino acids (except glycine) are optically active.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Peptide bond formation: Amino acids can connect with a peptide bond involving their amino and carboxylate groups. A covalent bond formed between the alpha-amino group of one amino acid and an alpha-carboxyl group of other forming -CO-NH-linkage. Peptide bonds are planar and partially ionic.</p>
</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="chemical-properties">Chemical Properties<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#chemical-properties" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="1-zwitter-ionic-property">1. Zwitter ionic property<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#1-zwitter-ionic-property" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>A zwitterion is a molecule with functional groups, of which at least one has a positive and a negative electrical charge.</p>
<p>The net charge of the entire molecule is zero. Amino acids are the best-known examples of zwitterions. They contain an amine group (basic) and a carboxylic group (acidic). The -NH₂ group is the stronger base, and so it picks up H⁺ from the -COOH group to leave a zwitterion (illustrate formation of a zwitter ion)</p>
<p>The (neutral) zwitter ion is the usual form amino acids exist in solution.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="2-amphoteric-property">2. Amphoteric property<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2-amphoteric-property" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Amino acids are amphoteric in nature; that is, they act as both acids and base since due to the amine and carboxylic group present.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="3-ninhydrin-test">3. Ninhydrin test<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#3-ninhydrin-test" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Ninhydrin is a chemical compound with the formula C₉H₆O₄; IUPAC name: 2,2-dihydroxyindane-1,3-dione.</p>
<p>When 1 ml of Ninhydrin solution is added to a 1 ml protein solution and heated, the formation of a violet/blue color indicates the presence of α-amino acids. The development of a deep blue colour indicates the presence of ammonia, primary/secondary amines, or amino acids in the analyte</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="4-xanthoproteic-test">4. Xanthoproteic test<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#4-xanthoproteic-test" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<p>5</p>
<p>studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by PATRICK NGOBIRO (<a href="mailto:patrickngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">patrickngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>The xanthoproteic test is performed for the detection of aromatic amino acids (tyrosine, tryptophan, and phenylalanine) in a protein solution. The nitration of benzoid radicals present in the amino acid chain occurs due to reaction with nitric acid, giving the solution yellow coloration.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="5-reaction-with-sanger-s-reagent">5. Reaction with Sanger's reagent<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#5-reaction-with-sanger-s-reagent" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Sanger's reagent (1-fluoro-2, 4-dinitrobenzene) reacts with a free amino group in the peptide chain in a mild alkaline medium under cold conditions.</p>
<p>1-Fluoro-2,4-dinitrobenzene (DNFB), also known as Sanger's reagent, was first used by Sanger to detect free amino acids of Insulin. DNFB undergoes nucleophilic aromatic substitution with the N-terminal amino group of a peptide or protein. After hydrolysis of the peptide or protein, the individual amino acids separate and only the labeled N-terminal amino acid can be detected by a colorimetric detection at specific wavelength. DNFB is hence used in protein sequencing to determine N-terminal amino acid.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="6-reaction-with-nitrous-acid">6. Reaction with nitrous acid<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#6-reaction-with-nitrous-acid" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Nitrous acid reacts with the amino group to liberate nitrogen and form the corresponding hydroxyl.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="classification-of-amino-acids">Classification of amino acids<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#classification-of-amino-acids" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Amino acids can be classified in various ways:</p>
<ol>
<li>On the basis of R-group</li>
<li>On the basis of nutrition.</li>
<li>Metabolic fate</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="classification-on-the-basis-of-r-group">Classification on the basis of R-group<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#classification-on-the-basis-of-r-group" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ol>
<li>Nonpolar, aliphatic amino acids: The R groups in this class of amino acids are nonpolar and hydrophobic. Glycine, Alanine, Valine, leucine, Isoleucine, Methionine, Proline.</li>
<li>Aromatic amino acids: Phenylalanine, tyrosine, and tryptophan, with their aromatic side chains, are relatively nonpolar (hydrophobic). All can participate in hydrophobic interactions.</li>
<li>Polar, Uncharged amino acids: The R groups of these amino acids are more soluble in water, or more hydrophilic, than those of the nonpolar amino acids, because they contain functional groups that form hydrogen bonds with water. This class of amino acids includes serine, threonine, cysteine, asparagine, and glutamine.</li>
</ol>
<p>Downloaded by PATRICK NGOBIRO (<a href="mailto:patrickngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">patrickngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ol start="4">
<li>
<p>Acidic amino acids: Amino acids in which R-group is acidic or negatively charged. Glutamic acid and Aspartic acid</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Basic amino acids: Amino acids in which R-group is basic or positively charged. Lysine, Arginine, Histidine</p>
</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="classification-on-the-basis-of-nutrition">Classification on the basis of nutrition<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#classification-on-the-basis-of-nutrition" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ol>
<li>Essential amino acids (Nine)</li>
</ol>
<p>Nine (9) amino acids cannot be synthesized in the body and, therefore, must be present in the diet in order for protein synthesis to occur.</p>
<p>These essential amino acids are histidine, isoleucine, leucine, lysine, methionine, phenylalanine, threonine, tryptophan, and valine.</p>
<ol start="2">
<li>Non-essential amino acids (Eleven)</li>
</ol>
<p>These amino acids can be synthesized in the body itself and hence not necessarily need to be acquired through diet.</p>
<p>Arginine, glutamine, tyrosine, cysteine, glycine, proline, serine, ornithine, alanine, asparagine, and aspartate.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="classification-on-the-basis-of-metabolic-fate">Classification on the basis of metabolic fate<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#classification-on-the-basis-of-metabolic-fate" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ol>
<li>
<p>Glucogenic amino acids: These amino acids serve as precursor's gluconeogenesis for glucose formation. Glycine, alanine, serine, aspartic acid, asparagine, glutamic acid, glutamine, proline, valine, methionine, cysteine, histidine, and arginine.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Ketogenic amino acids: These amino acids are breakdown to form ketone bodies. Leucine and Lysine.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Both glucogenic and ketogenic amino acids: These amino acids breakdown to form precursors for both ketone bodies and glucose. Isoleucine, Phenylalanine, Tryptophan, and tyrosine.</p>
</li>
</ol>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<p>5</p>
<p>studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by PATRICK NGOBIRO (<a href="mailto:patrickngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">patrickngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="amino-acid-metabolism">AMINO ACID METABOLISM<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#amino-acid-metabolism" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Average adult humans require approximately 60 -100 grams of dietary protein per day.</p>
<p>Amino acids are produced by digestion of dietary proteins in the intestines, absorbed through the intestinal epithelial cells, and enter the blood.</p>
<p>Various cells take up these amino acids, which enter the cellular amino acid pools.</p>
<p>Amino acids are used for the synthesis of proteins and other nitrogen-containing compounds, or their carbon skeletons are oxidized for energy or used for the synthesis of glucose during hypoglycemia.</p>
<p>The body maintains a relatively large free amino acid pool in the blood (approximately 35-65 mg/decilitre), even during fasting; tissues have continuous access to individual amino acids for the synthesis of proteins and essential amino acid derivatives, such as neurotransmitters. The amino acid pool also provides the liver with substrates for gluconeogenesis and ketogenesis. The free amino acid pool is derived from dietary amino acids and the proteolysis of body proteins. All useful nitrogen in the body is derived from amino acids.</p>
<p>All nitrogen-containing compounds of the body are synthesized from amino acids - cellular proteins, hormones (e.g., thyroxine, epinephrine, and insulin), neurotransmitters, creatine phosphate, and heme in haemoglobin and cytochromes, melanin, purine and pyrimidine bases.</p>
<p>Proteins in the body are constantly synthesized and degraded, partially draining and refilling the cellular amino acid pools.</p>
<p>As a source of energy, amino acid carbon skeletons are directly oxidized, or, in the starved state, converted to glucose and ketone bodies, and then oxidized.</p>
<p>Nitrogen must be removed (deamination) before the carbon skeletons of amino acids are oxidized.</p>
<p>The liver is the major site of amino acid oxidation, but most tissues can oxidize the branched chain amino acids (i.e., leucine, isoleucine, and valine).</p>
<p>Downloaded by PATRICK NGOBIRO (<a href="mailto:patrickngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">patrickngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>Most of the carbons from amino acid degradation are converted to pyruvate, intermediates of the TCA cycle or acetyl CoA.</p>
<p><strong>NB</strong>: Pyruvate is a key intersection in the network of metabolic pathways. Pyruvate can be converted into carbohydrates via gluconeogenesis, to fatty acids or energy through acetyl-CoA, to the amino acid alanine, and to ethanol.</p>
<p>During fasting, these carbons are converted to glucose in the liver and kidney, or to ketone bodies in the liver. In the well fed state, they may be used for lipogenesis and amino acid nitrogen which forms ammonia which is toxic.</p>
<p>The liver is the major site of amino acid metabolism in the body and the major site of urea synthesis. The liver is also the major site of amino acid degradation, and partially oxidizes most amino acids, converting the carbon skeleton to glucose, ketone bodies, or <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><msub><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">C</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">O</mi></mrow><mn>2</mn></msub></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\mathrm{CO}_{2}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.8333em; vertical-align: -0.15em;"></span><span class="mord"><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathrm">CO</span></span><span class="msupsub"><span class="vlist-t vlist-t2"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.3011em;"><span style="top: -2.55em; margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 2.7em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mtight"><span class="mord mtight">2</span></span></span></span></span><span class="vlist-s">​</span></span><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.15em;"><span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span>. In liver, the urea cycle converts ammonia and the amino groups from amino acids to urea, which is non-toxic, water-soluble, and easily excreted in the urine.</p>
<p>Nitrogen derived from amino acid catabolism in other tissues is transported to the liver, in large part, as alanine or glutamine, the major transporters of ammonia in the blood.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="amino-acid-metabolism-disorders">Amino Acid Metabolism Disorders<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#amino-acid-metabolism-disorders" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Amino acid metabolism disorders are hereditary metabolic disorders. Hereditary disorders occur when parents pass the defective genes that cause these disorders to their children. In most hereditary metabolic disorders, both parents of the affected child carry a copy of the abnormal gene. Because usually two copies of the abnormal gene are necessary for the disorder to occur, usually neither parent has the disorder. Some hereditary metabolic disorders are X-linked, which means only one copy of the abnormal gene can cause the disorder in boys. Amino acids are the building blocks of proteins and have many functions in the body. Hereditary disorders of amino acid processing (metabolism) can result from defects either in the breakdown of amino acids or in the body's ability to get amino acids into cells. Because these disorders cause symptoms early in life, newborns are routinely screened for several common amino acid disorders. Newborns should be commonly screened for</p>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<p>6</p>
<p>studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by PATRICK NGOBIRO (<a href="mailto:patrickngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">patrickngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>Phenylketonuria</li>
<li>Tyrosinaemia</li>
<li>Homocystinuria</li>
<li>Maple syrup urine disease</li>
</ul>
<p>Disorders that affect the metabolism of amino acids include phenylketonuria, tyrosinaemia, homocystinuria, non-ketotic hyperglycemia, and maple syrup urine disease. These disorders are autosomal recessive, and all may be diagnosed by analyzing amino acid concentrations in body fluids.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="phenylketonuria-pku">Phenylketonuria (PKU)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#phenylketonuria-pku" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Phenylketonuria (PKU) is caused by decreased activity of the enzyme phenylalanine hydroxylase. Phenylalanine hydroxylase (PAH), is an enzyme that converts the amino acid phenylalanine to tyrosine, a precursor (something that precedes: In biological processes, a substance from which another, usually more active or mature substance is formed) of several important hormones and skin, hair, and eye pigments.</p>
<p>Decreased PAH activity results in, accumulation of phenylalanine and a decreased amount of tyrosine and other metabolites. Phenylalanine is consequently converted to phenyl pyruvic acid (a ketone) which accumulates in the blood hence causing PKU. Persistent high levels of phenyl pyruvic acid in the blood in turn result in progressive developmental delay, a small head circumference, behaviour disturbances, and seizures. Due to a decreased amount of the pigment melanin, persons with PKU tend to have lighter features, such as blond hair and blue eyes.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="tyrosinemia">Tyrosinemia<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#tyrosinemia" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Tyrosinemia is a genetic disorder characterized by disruptions in the multistep process that breaks down the amino acid tyrosine, a building block of most proteins. If untreated, tyrosine and its byproducts build up in tissues and organs, which can lead to serious health problems.</p>
<p>Classic (hepatorenal or type I) tyrosinemia is caused by a deficiency of fumaryl acetoacetate hydrolase enzyme (FAH), the last enzyme in tyrosine catabolism.</p>
<p>Symptoms and physical findings associated with tyrosinemia type I appear in the first months of life and include failure to gain weight and grow at the expected rate (failure to thrive), fever,</p>
<p>Downloaded by PATRICK NGOBIRO (<a href="mailto:patrickngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">patrickngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>diarrhea, vomiting, an abnormally enlarged liver (hepatomegaly), and yellowing of the skin and the whites of the eyes</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="homocystinuria">Homocystinuria<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#homocystinuria" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Homocystinuria (HCU) is a rare but potentially serious inherited condition. It means the body can't process the amino acid methionine. This causes a harmful build-up of substances in the blood and urine</p>
<p>Homocystinuria is caused by a defect in cystathionine beta-synthase (or β-synthase), an enzyme that participates in the metabolism of methionine, which leads to an accumulation of homocysteine. Symptoms include a pronounced flush of the cheeks, a tall, thin frame, lens dislocation, vascular disease, and thinning of the bones (osteoporosis). Intellectual disability and psychiatric disorders also may be present</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="non-ketotic-hyperglycinemia">Non-ketotic hyperglycinemia<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#non-ketotic-hyperglycinemia" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Non-ketotic hyperglycinemia (NKH) is a rare, genetic, metabolic disorder caused by a defect in the enzyme system that breaks down the amino acid glycine, resulting in an accumulation of glycine in the body's tissues and fluids.</p>
<p>Non-ketotic hyperglycinemia is caused by elevated levels of the neurotransmitter glycine in the central nervous system, which in turn are caused by a defect in the enzyme system responsible for cleaving the amino acid glycine.</p>
<p>Drugs that block the action of glycine (e.g., dextromethorphan), a low-protein diet, and glycine-scavenging medications (e.g., sodium benzoate) may ease symptoms, but there is no cure for this severe condition</p>
<p>Non-ketotic hyperglycinemia is characterized by seizures, low muscle tone, hiccups, breath holding, and severe developmental impairment.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="maple-syrup-urine-disease-msud">Maple syrup urine disease (MSUD)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#maple-syrup-urine-disease-msud" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Maple syrup urine disease (MSUD) is a rare but serious inherited condition. It means the body cannot process certain amino acids (the "building blocks" of protein), causing a harmful build-up of substances in the blood and urine. Normally, our bodies break down protein foods such as meat and fish into amino acids</p>
<p>Maple syrup urine disease (MSUD) is a disorder of branched-chain amino acid metabolism that leads to the accumulation of leucine, isoleucine, valine and their corresponding oxoacids in</p>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<p>8</p>
<p>studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by PATRICK NGOBIRO (<a href="mailto:patrickngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">patrickngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>body fluids one result being a characteristic maple syrup smell to the urine of some patients. The classic form of MSUD presents in infancy with lethargy and progressive neurological deterioration characterized by seizures and coma. Unlike most organic acidemias, prominent acidemia is rare. Treatment involves restricting proteins and feeding with formulas deficient in the branched-chain amino acids. Persons with MSUD may have intellectual disability despite therapy, but early and careful treatment can result in normal intellectual development. Milder forms of MSUD may be treated with simple protein restriction or administration of thiamin (vitamin B₁)</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="alkaptonuria">Alkaptonuria<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#alkaptonuria" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Alkaptonuria, or "black urine disease", is a very rare inherited disorder that prevents the body from fully breaking down two protein building blocks (amino acids) called tyrosine and phenylalanine. It results in a build-up of a chemical called homogentisic acid in the body. Alkaptonuria is a rare inherited disorder. It occurs when your body cannot produce enough of an enzyme called homogentisic dioxygenase (HGD). This enzyme is used to break down a toxic substance called homogentisic acid.</p>
<p>In alkaptonuria the body is unable to utilize homogentisate, an intermediate of phenylalanine and tyrosine metabolism. This metabolic aberration results in accumulation of homogentisate and its oxidation products in the body and their abnormal excretion in the urine. Due to oxidation of homogentisate, the urine becomes dark brown or black when exposed to air. In childhood, alkaptonuria is usually benign and there are no associated physical disturbances. In older individuals the disease may result in osteoporosis, arthritis, and changes in pigmentation. The occurrence of black urine has been known for centuries.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="albinism">Albinism<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#albinism" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Albinism is a rare group of genetic disorders that cause the skin, hair, or eyes to have little or no black color. Albinism is also associated with vision problems.</p>
<p>Albinism, the most easily recognized error of amino acid metabolism, results from an inherited inability of the body to form the pigment melanin. Ordinarily, tyrosine is converted to 3, 4-dihydroxyphenylalanine (dopa) which in turn undergoes numerous chemical changes to form melanin. Grossly, albinism may be subdivided into total albinism and partial albinism or partial baldness. In the former there is a lack of pigmentation throughout the entire body while in partial</p>
<p>Downloaded by PATRICK NGOBIRO (<a href="mailto:patrickngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">patrickngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>albinism the absence of pigment is limited to well-defined areas of the skin. The lack of pigmentation in total albinos is so striking that their existence has been recorded since ancient times.</p>
<p>NOTE: Albinism is a genetic disorder that results in decreased production of a pigment called melanin in the skin, hair, and eyes, resulting in light color or no color. There are several genetic mutations that cause albinism. This condition occurs throughout the world; it affects 1 in 20,000 Americans and is more common in other parts of the world, such as in Zimbabwe, Africa, where 1 in 1,000 people have some form of albinism. Currently there is no cure for albinism, however it can be managed using sunscreen, protective clothing, eyeglasses, magnifying glasses, or eye surgery for eye abnormalities. The impact of this condition on people's lives can be devastating, so it is important to foster a positive and tolerant environment to promote the emotional and mental well-being of those who are affected.</p>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="carbohydrates">CARBOHYDRATES<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#carbohydrates" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="unit-objectives">Unit Objectives<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#unit-objectives" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
